It is only the various coloured parts that masquerade as the whole colour of the cloth. Acceptance of this view-point poses a problem to Annambhatta. If the cloth as a whole does not possess any colour of its own, then cloth can never become an object of visual perception; for, a substance without some manifest colour cannot be visually perceived. But it is perceived; then, which colour is it? It cannot be the colour of any of its component parts for the reason mentioned above. Therefore, the colour of the cloth must be the one which is the total effect of all the colours of its component parts. This total effect of all the colours is a simple distinctive colour over and above the colours of its component parts. And, according to Annambatta, this distinctive colour is known as citra rūpa] Objection: If the above argument is accepted, it will lead to a conclusion that a piece of cloth is not possessing any colour. A piece of cloth possesses colour but not directly. There is close contact between cloth and its threads (in the same sense in which a clay-made-pot is accepted to have inseparable contact with clay); so, here too, cloth possesses the colour on account of its being samaveta (inherent or inseparably united) in its components, ie threads in which colour exists. Reply: Accepting such a condition for perceptibility is cumbersome, and is not preferable to the usual reasoning of rūpavatva (having colour). Therefore, we must recognise an independent variegated colour to account for the perceptibility of a variegated piece of cloth. Non-pervasiveness (avyāpya vrttitvam) of a thing is the characteristic property of being existent in the same substratum with its absolute negation. The Text explains the substratum of colour: Prthivi.... The substratum is again divided by the Text: Tatra....
Verse 20
2. Taste (rasa): रसनग्राह्यो गुणो रसः । स च मधुराम्ललवणकटुकषायतिक्तभेदात् षड्विधः । पृथिवीजलवृत्तिः । तत्र पृथिव्यां षड्विधः । जले मधुर एव ॥ २ ॥
English Paraphrase
2. Savour is the quality apprehended by the gustatory organ; it is of six varieties: sweet, acid, saline, pungent, astringent and bitter. Taste subsists in earth and water. Of these, earth has all the six varieties while water has only the sweet variety.
Sanskrit Commentary
रसं लक्षयति । रसनेति । रसत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । रसस्याश्रयमाह । पृथिवीति । आश्रयं विभज्य दर्शयति । तत्रेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The text gives the definition of savour: rasanā.... To avoid the defect of overapplicability in the generic attribute of savour (rasatva), the word quality is inserted in the definition. The substratum of savour is shown by the Text: Prthivi.... The substratum is further explained by its division: pṛthivyām....
Verse 21
3. Odour (gandha): घ्राणग्राह्यो गुणो गन्धः । स द्विविधः सुरभिरसुरभिश्च । पृथिवीमात्रवृत्तिः ॥ ३ ॥
English Paraphrase
3. Odour is the quality perceived by the olfactory organ. It is twofold; fragrant and foul. It subsists in earth alone.
Sanskrit Commentary
गन्धं लक्षयति । घ्राणेति । गन्धत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines odour: ghrāna.... To avoid the defect of overapplicability in the generic attribute of odour (gandhatva), the term quality is inserted in the definition.
Verse 22
4. Touch (Sparśa): त्वगिन्द्रियमात्रग्राह्यो गुणः स्पर्शः । स च त्रिविधः शीतोष्णानुष्णाशीतभेदात् । पृथिव्यप्तेजोवायुवृत्तिः । तत्र शीतो जले । उष्णस्तेजसि । अनुष्णाशीतः पृथिवीवाय्वोः ॥ ४ ॥
English Paraphrase
4. Touch is the quality perceived by the tactile organ alone. It is threefold: cool, hot, and lukewarm. It resides in water, earth, light and air. Of these, cool touch resides in water, hot touch in light and lukewarm in earth and air.
Sanskrit Commentary
स्पर्श लक्षयति । त्वगिति । स्पर्शत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । संयोगादावतिव्या- प्तिवाराणाय मात्रपदम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The definition of touch is given in Text: tvagindriya. To avoid overapplicability of the definition in the generic attribute of touch, the word quality is inserted in the definition. Alone is inserted in the definition to avoid overapplicability in conjunction.[49. The insertion of alone in the definition is intended to avoid the defect of ativyapti in contact of the tactile sense with a tactile substance. The tactile sense is supposed to apprehend only touch, either warm or cold. According to the Vaiśeşika Ontology, contact also is a guna; but contact is not the same as warm or cold. So, insertion of alone removes this difficulty, as contact is apprehended by the visual organ also]
Verse 23
5. The Theory of Pāka: रूपादिचतुष्टयं पृथिव्यां पाकजमनित्यं च । अन्यत्र अपाकजं नित्यमनित्यं च । नित्यगतं नित्यम् अनित्यगतमनित्यम् ॥ ५ ॥
English Paraphrase
5. The four qualities beginning with colour are produced in earth through the application of heat, and are non-eternal. In other substances (ie in water, light and air) they are not produced by the application of heat (ie natural) and are eternal and also non-eternal; in eternals (ie in atoms) they are eternal; and in non-eternals (ie in products) they are non-eternal.
Sanskrit Commentary
पाकजमिति । पाकस्तेजः संयोगः । तेन पूर्वरूपं नश्यति रूपान्तरमुत्पद्यत इत्यर्थः । तत्र परमाणुष्वेव पाको न द्वयणुकादौ । आमनिक्षिप्ते घटे परमाणुषु रूपान्तरोत्पत्तौ श्यामघटनाशे पुनद्वर्यणुकादिक्रमेण रक्तघटोत्पत्तिः । तत्र परमाणवः समवायिकारणम् । तेजः संयोगोऽसमवायिकारणम् । अदृष्टादिकं निमित्तकारणम् । द्वचणुकादिरूपे कारणरूपमसमवायिकारणमिति पीलुपाकवादिनो वैशेषिकाः । पूर्वघटस्य नाशं विनैव अवयविनि अवयवेषु च परमाणुपर्यन्तेषु युगपद्रूपान्तरोत्पत्तिरिति पिठरपाकवादिनो तैयायिकाः । अत एव पार्थिवपरमाणुरूपादिकमनित्यमित्यर्थः । अन्यत्र जलादावित्यर्थः । नित्यगतमिति । परमाणुगतमित्यर्थः । अनित्यगतमिति । द्वयणुकादिगतभित्यर्थः । रूपादिचतुष्टयम् उद्भूतं प्रत्यक्षम् । अनुद्भूतमप्रत्यक्षम् । उद्भूतत्वं प्रत्यक्षत्वप्रयोजको धर्मः । तदभावोऽनुद्भूतत्वम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Contact with light is pāka. The meaning is that by the contact of light with earth the colour (of the earthen product) existing prior to its contact with light gets destroyed and an altogether different colour is produced in its place. This process of baking takes place in atoms only and not in dyads etc. When an unbaked jar is baked in mild heat, the jar of black colour gets disintegrated (down to the stage of atoms along with the qualities in those atoms); and a different colour is produced (in those atoms). Again, by integration of (fresh) dyads and triads, etc a new jar of red colour comes into being. Here atoms form the inherent cause (of the new red colour) and the contact of light forms the non-inherent cause of (the new red colour of the atoms), and the unseen results of the actions of the individual soul (adrsta) for whom it is to be created, constitute the instrumental cause. The Vaiśeşika theorists known as Pīlupākavādins hold the view that the effect of colour etc is produced in atoms only and not in dyads etc. But the Naiyāyikas known as Pitharapākavādins hold the view that the contact of light destroys only the black colour of the jar down to the stage of atoms without destroying the old black jar itself and while producing simultaneously an altogether different red colour in the component parts down to the stage of the atoms. This is why it is said that colour etc subsisting in the atoms of earth are non-eternal. By anyatra in the text, other substances like water, light and air are meant. The word nityagatam indicates (the four qualities) subsisting in the atoms. By anityagatam it is implied that (the four qualities) subsisting in dyads etc are non-eternal. The four qualities beginning with colour possess manifested perceptibility. Those which do not possess manifest perceptibility are imperceptible. Manifest perceptibility is the attribute which helps in direct perception and the lack of this attribute is Non-perceptibility. [50. According to the Vaisesikas, the change that takes place due to application of heat (pakaja change) is affected directly in the ultimate (ie atomic) constituents of gross earth-substance. He gives an example of an unbaked jar. When it is subjected to some degree of heat, its colour changes from black to red under the influence of heat. This change we see in the gross jar is because of the change that undergoes directly in the atoms which are the ultimate constituents of the gross object. Tejas-particles induce motion in earth paramāņus; this motion effects the separation of atoms in a dvyanuka. With the disintegration of dvyanuka, tryanuka also disintegrates, which finally leads to the disintegration of the gross jar itself which is the ultimate effect in this causal-series. Thus, it is the destruction of contact of paramāņu with Tejas particles that finally results in the destruction of the jar this is known as pākaja-effect induced by pāka-kriyā. As the Paramāņu separates from dvyanuka, its former black colour gets destroyed and in its place appears red colour under the influence of contact with Tejas. These paramāņus with their changed colour are the starting point of another series of causal changes which finally culminates in the emergence of a red jar. Here, the change is only in the quality of atoms and not in the substance as such because a paramāņu is indivisible and changeless. This change of quality is a positive effect (bhāva kārya) and has its samavāyi kāraṇa in the paramāņu as a dravya or substance; its asamavāyi kāraṇa is in its contact with Tejas particles or pāka. This contact inheres in a paramāņu. The nimitta karana or the instrumental cause, is in the Adrsta of the jīva for whom the jar is being produced. The change of colour in a paramāņu is due to paka consisting in asamavāyi kāraṇa. The red colour in a dvyanuka is due to the red colour of its constituent atoms and the samavāyi kāraṇa of the red colour of a dvyanuka is the dvyanuka itself and the nimitta karana is the adrsta that was operative in the case of the change of quality in the paramāņu. While the Naiyāyikas accept the Vaisesika theory that the four specific qualities of earth substance (viz rūpa, rasa, gandha and sparśa) are affected by the application of heat (paka kriyā), they differ from the Vaiśeşikas with regard to the process of change in colour from black to red. According to the latter, pāka kriyā (ie change in colour due to heat) takes place only in earth paramāņus. But the former hold that like the atoms, the jar too undergoes a similar change at the same time]
Verse 24
6. Number-Sankhyā: एकत्वादिव्यवहारहेतुः संख्या । सा नवद्रव्यवृत्तिः एकत्वादिपरार्धपर्यन्ता । एकत्वं नित्यमनित्यं च । नित्यगतं नित्यम् । अनित्यगतमनित्यम् । द्वित्वादिकं तु सर्वत्राऽनित्यमेव ॥ ६ ॥
English Paraphrase
6. Number is the cause of common usage of (enumerative) expressions like one, two, etc. It is found in all the nine substances. It starts from one and ends with parārdha. Oneness is eternal and non-eternal (ie eternal in atoms and non-eternal in products); eternal in eternal substances and non-eternal in non-eternals. Number two and higher numbers are only non-eternals everywhere.
Sanskrit Commentary
संख्यां लक्षयति । एकेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines number: Ekatvādi.... (Parārdha is the highest number equivalent to a lakh of lakhs of crores, which constitutes half of the life-span of Brahma, the creator.)
Verse 25
7. Dimension (Parimāṇam): मानव्यवहारासाधारणकारणं परिमाणम् । नवद्रव्यवृत्तिः । तच्चतुर्विधम् । अणु महत् दीर्घ ह्रस्वं चेति ॥ ७ ॥
English Paraphrase
7. Dimension is the special cause of the common usage of words pertaining to measurement. It is found in all the nine substances. And, it is of four varieties: atomic, large, long, and short.
Sanskrit Commentary
परिमाणं लक्षयति । मानेति । परिमाणं विभजते । तच्चेति । भावप्रधानो निर्देशः । अणुत्वं, महत्त्वं, दीर्घत्वं, ह्रस्वत्वं चेत्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines dimension: Māna.... Dimension is divided: taccha.... Aṇu, etc in the Text indicate aṇutva etc that is to say, minuteness, largeness, length and shortness. [51. Further, parimāņa is a real feature of a thing and is not something just fancied about; eg when we say, this thing is big, we really intend to describe the thing with certain specification with reference to its size which is not a mere subjective whim but an objective reality. As Realists, the Vaisesikas term this feature of a substance as parimāna or dimension. To exclude Iśvara, time, dik and adrsta which are the common causes of all products, the word 'special' is inserted in the definition. The dimension of atoms known as pārimandalya - infinitesimality and the dimension of vibhu-dravyas (all-pervasive substances), are eternal; all the rest are non-eternal]
Verse 26
8. Distinctness (Pṛthaktvam): पृथग्व्यवहारासाधारणकारणं पृथक्त्वम् । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ ८ ॥
English Paraphrase
8. Distinctness is the special cause of conventional expressions such as this is distinct from that. It is found in all the nine substances.
Sanskrit Commentary
पृथक्त्वं लक्षयति । पृथगिति । इदमस्मात्पृथक् इति व्यवहारकारणं पृथक्त्वमित्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines distinctness: prthak.... That which causes the expression of distinctness of one object from another is severalty. [52. It has been argued that distinctness can as well be considered a kind of abhāva (absence) known as anyonyābhāva (ie mutual absence). Vaiśeşikas do not accept this theory as it would go against their enumeration of qualities as twenty four; further, the apprehension this is distinct from that is a positive experience; it being so, to consider it a mere absence is doing violence to our consciousness; hence, it is preferable to accept this awareness as a quality rather than as a kind of absence]
Verse 27
9. Conjunction (Samyoga): संयुक्तव्यवहारहेतुः संयोगः । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ ९ ॥
English Paraphrase
9. Conjunction is the special cause of conventional expressions of contact between two objects. It exists in all the substances.
Sanskrit Commentary
संयोगं लक्षयति । संयुक्तेति । इमौ संयुक्तौ इति व्यवहारहेतुरित्यर्थः । संख्यादिलक्षणे सर्वत्र दिक्कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय असाधारणेति विशेषणीयम् । संयोगो द्विविधः कर्मजः संयोगजश्वेति । आद्यो हस्तक्रियया हस्तपुस्तकसंयोगः । द्वितीयो हस्तपुस्तकसंयोगात्कायपुस्तकसंयोगः । अव्याप्यवृत्तिः संयोगः । स्वात्यन्ताभावसमाना- धिकरणत्वमव्याप्यवृत्तित्वम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines conjunction: samyukta.... The expression which causes the idea: these are in conjunction with each other, is conjunction. Everywhere the term special ought to be given while denoting the characteristic mark of number etc in order to remove the defect of overapplicability in space, time, etc. Conjunction is of two varieties: born of action and of contact. The former arises when a book comes in contact with hand, due to the activity of the hand in grasping a book. The second variety is the contact which arises between one's body taken as a whole and a book, when the book is in contact with one's hand. Conjunction is a non-pervasive (quality). Non-pervasiveness in a thing is the characteristic quality of being co-existent in the same substratum with its absolute negation. [53. According to the Vaiśeşikas, conjunction (samyoga) is a quality inhering in a substance. It is an objective reality due to which the expression signifying conjunction is possible, eg when the book is on the table. It is again of two kinds, born of action or direct, and born of contact itself or indirect; eg the contact of a hand and a book is born of action and is the direct samyoga. Indirect samyoga is due to samyoga itself, that is to say, the direct contact of a book with the hand indirectly results in the contact of the book with the entire body of which the hand is only a part. Further, according to the Vaiśeşikas, samyoga is a avyāpya vṛtti, ie non-pervasive property. This non-pervasiveness is when the thing and its absence have the same substratum; eg when the hand is in contact with the table, the absence of that contact also is in the table (taking the entire table as the substratum), or, it also might be that even when the hand is in contact with the table, there is the awareness that the hand is not a part of the table. Thus, avyāpya comes to be defined as being co-existent in the same locus where there is also the absolute negation]
Verse 28
10. Disjunction (Vibhāga): संयोगनाशको गुणो विभागः । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ १० ॥
English Paraphrase
10. Disjunction is the quality which destroys conjunction. It resides in all the nine substances.
Sanskrit Commentary
विभागं लक्षयति । संयोगेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुण इति । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय संयोगनाशक इति । विभागोऽपि द्विविधः कर्मजो विभागजश्वेति । आद्यो हस्तपुस्तकविभागः । द्वितीयो हस्तपुस्तकविभागात्कायपुस्तकविभागः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines disjunction: samyoga.... The word quality is inserted in the definition to avoid over-applicability in time etc. To avoid overapplicability in colour etc the term destroyer of contact is used in the definition. Disjunction is also of two kinds: born of action and born of disjunction. The first variety is the disjunction of a book from the hand caused by the action of the hand (in throwing away the book). The second variety is the disjunction of the book from the body caused by the disjunction of the book from the hand.
Verse 29
11. Remoteness - Paratva and Proximity (aparatva): परापरव्यवहारासाधारणकारणे परत्वापरत्वे । पृथिव्यादिचतुष्टय मनोवृत्तिनी । ते द्विविधे दिक्कृते कालकृते च । दूरस्थे दिक्कृतं परत्वं । समीपस्थे दिक्कृतमपरत्वम् । ज्येष्ठे कालकृतं परत्वम् । कनिष्ठे कालकृतमपरत्वम् ॥ ११ ॥
English Paraphrase
11. Remoteness and proximity are the special causes of common usage of words expressing remoteness and proximity. They reside in four substances beginning with earth, and also in mind. Each of them is of two varieties: spatial and temporal. In things existing at a distance, spatial remoteness is found; in things near, spatial proximity is found; in an elderly person, temporal remoteness is found; in a younger person, temporal proximity is found.
Sanskrit Commentary
परव्यवहारासाधारणकारणं परत्वम् । परत्वापरत्वयोर्लक्षणमाह । परापरेति । अपरव्यवहारासाधारणकारणमपरत्वम् इत्यर्थः । ते विभजते । ते द्विविधे इति । दिक्कृतयोरुदाहरणमाह । दूरस्थइति । कालकृते उदाहरति । ज्येष्ठ इति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines remoteness and proximity: Para.... The special cause of expression of posteriority is remoteness. The special cause of expression of priority is proximity. Remoteness and proximity are divided by the Text: te dvividhe.... The example for remoteness and proximity caused by spatial distance and nearness is shown: dūrasthe.... The example for remoteness and proximity caused by temporal distance and nearness is shown in the Text: jyesthe....
Verse 30
12. Heaviness (Gurutvam): आद्यपतनासमवायिकारणं गुरुत्वम् । पृथिवीजलवृत्ति ॥ १२ ॥
English Paraphrase
12. Heaviness is the non-intimate cause of the first down-ward motion in a falling substance. It resides in earth and water.
Sanskrit Commentary
गुरुत्वं लक्षयति । आद्येति । द्वितीयादिपतनस्य वेगासमवायिकारणत्वाद्वेगेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय आद्येति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines Heaviness: ādya.... Velocity is the non-intimate cause of the second and subsequent acts of falling of a substance. Therefore, to avoid overapplicability (of the definition) in velocity, the word ādya is used in the definition.
Verse 31
13. Fluidity - (dravatvam): आद्यस्यन्दनासमवायिकारणं द्रवत्वं । पृथिव्यप्तेजोवृत्ति । त‌द्विविधं, सांसिद्धिकं नैमित्तिकं च । सांसिद्धिकं जले । नैमित्तिकं पृथिवीतेजसोः । पृथिव्यां घृतादावग्नि संयोगजं द्रवत्वम् । तेजसि सुवर्णादौ ॥ १३ ॥
English Paraphrase
13. Fluidity is the non-intimate cause of the first flow (of a liquid substance). It resides in earth, water and light. It is of two varieties: natural and artificial. In water, natural fluidity is found. In earth and light, artificial fluidity is found. In earth artificial fluidity is generated through the contact of fire with ghee. Artificial fluidity is found in gold etc belonging to light.
Sanskrit Commentary
द्रवत्वं लक्षयति । आद्येति । स्यन्दनं प्रस्रवणम् । तेजःसंयोगजं नैमित्तिकम् । तद्भिन्नं सांसिद्धिकम् । पृथिव्यां नैमित्तिकमुदाहरति । घृतादाविति । तेजसि तदाह । सुवर्णादाविति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines fluidity: ādyasyandana.... Flow is the act of flowing of a liquid substance. The fluidity generated through the contact of light is artificial fluidity. The one other than this is the natural fluidity. An example of artificial fluidity in earth is given in ghee coming in contact with fire. In the same way, artificial fluidity in light is exemplified in gold. (The word adya is inserted in the definition to exclude velocity which is the non-intimate cause of the second and all subsequent acts of flowing).
Verse 32
14. Viscidity (Snehah): चूर्णादिपिण्डीभावहेतुर्गुणः स्नेहः । जलमात्रवृत्तिः ॥ १४ ॥
English Paraphrase
14. Viscidity is the quality which causes the agglutination of powder etc. It exists in water only.
Sanskrit Commentary
स्नेहं लक्षयति । चूर्णेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय पिण्डीभावेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines viscidity: curna.... The word quality is inserted in the definition to exclude time etc (which are the common causes of all effects). To avoid the defect of over-applicability in colour etc the word Pindībhāva etc is used in the definition. [54. Sneha is the special quality which helps solidification by bringing about a peculiar combination among particles of powder. To the argument that fluidity is the agglutinating agent and not sneha, Naiyāyikas answer that if it is so, liquefied gold should be the cause of agglutination of powder etc. But liquefied gold does not seem to possess this quality of agglutination. So, the agent that causes agglutination is sneha]
Verse 33
15. Sound (Sabdah): श्रोत्रग्राह्यो गुणः शब्दः, आकाशमात्रवृत्तिः । स द्विविधः, ध्वन्यात्मकः वर्णात्मकश्च । तत्र ध्वन्यात्मकः भेर्यादौ । वर्णात्मकः संस्कृतभाषादिरूपः ॥ १५ ॥
English Paraphrase
15. Sound is the quality apprehended by the organ of hearing. It subsists in ether alone. It is twofold: of the nature of inarticulate sound and of the nature of the alphabetic sound. Inarticulate sound is found in the sound produced by drums etc. The alphabetic sound is found in the form of languages like Sanskrit etc.
Sanskrit Commentary
शब्दं लक्षयति । श्रोत्रेति । शब्दत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय श्रोत्रेति । शब्दास्त्रिविधः संयोगजः, विभागजः, शब्दजश्वेति । तत्र आद्यो भेरीदण्डसंयोगजन्यः । द्वितीयो वंशे पाटचमाने दळद्वयविभागजन्यश्चटचटाशब्दः । भेर्यादिदेशमारभ्य श्रोत्रदेशपर्यन्तं द्वितीयादिशब्दाः शब्दजाः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines sound: śrotra.... The word quality is inserted in the definition to avoid the defect of overapplicability in soundness.[55. Since soundness too is perceived by the organ of hearing. Soundness being the generic attribute of sound (jāti), gets excluded by the insertion of quality in the definition] To avoid the defect of overapplicability in colour etc the word śrotra is used in the definition. Sound is of three kinds: born of conjunction; born of disjunction; and born of sound. The first kind of sound is produced by the contact of a stick with a drum; the decrepitating sound that is produced when a bamboo is halved is the second kind of sound; all subsequent sounds reaching the ear from the place where the sound is produced first, is the third kind of sound. [56. Sound is three-fold according to its source of origination. The peculiar samyoga between two material bodies (like a stick and a drum) produces one type of sound. The second variety of sound is produced due to vibhāga disjunction, eg when a bamboo is split. The third variety is when a series of sound is produced by the initial sound. When a drum is hit by a stick, it produces the first sound by means of samyoga. This first one generates the second sound which in turn generates the third sound and so on and on till the sound reaches the far-off auditory organs. This is the third variety of sound]
Verse 34
16. Cognition (Jñānam): सर्वव्यवहारहेतुर्गुणो बुद्धिर्ज्ञानम् । सा द्विविधा स्मृतिरनुभवश्च ॥ १६ ॥
English Paraphrase
16. Cognition is the quality which is the cause of all kinds of communications; and it is knowledge. It is two-fold: remembrance and apprehension.
Sanskrit Commentary
बुद्धेर्लक्षणमाह । सर्वव्यवहारेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुण इति । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय सर्वव्यवहार इति । जानामीत्यनुव्यवसायगम्यं ज्ञानमेव लक्षणमिति भावः । बुद्धिं विभजते । सेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The characteristic mark of cognition is given in the Text: sarva.... The word quality is inserted in the definition to avoid overapplicability in time etc. To avoid overapplicability in colour etc the term sarvavyavahāra... is inserted in the definition. The characteristic mark of cognition is: the immediate knowledge which becomes the subject (gamya) of the consciousness (anuvyavasāya) having the form: I know. [57. The word vyavahāra in the definition means apt usage of appropriate words. Vyavasaya jñāna is the simple knowledge of a thing, like, this is a jar. In Anuvyavasaya jñāna, the cognition this is a jar becomes the subject matter of the consciousness I know, ie it is visaya jñāna-visayakam jñānam. According to the Nyaya-Vaiśeşka doctrine, anuvyavasaya jñāna is subsequent to vyavasaya jñāna. According to them, the first cognitive state is not self-luminous, ie it does not become the subject of an internal perception. Perceiving a table is a state of cognition; at that moment there is no precise cognition I see a table. It is only at a subsequent instant that he has the consciousness that he sees a table. This subsequent subjective perception is anuvyavasaya jñāna]
Verse 35
17. Cognition is twofold: (i) Remembrance (Smrti) and (ii) Apprehension: (anubhava): (i) Remembrance (Smrti): संस्कारमात्रजन्यं ज्ञानं स्मृतिः ॥ १७ ॥
English Paraphrase
17. Remembrance is the knowledge born of mental impressions alone.
Sanskrit Commentary
स्मृतेर्लक्षणमाह । संस्कारेति । भावनाख्यः संस्कारः । संस्कारध्वंसेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय ज्ञानमिति । घटादिप्रत्यक्षेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय संस्कारजन्यमिति । अतिव्याप्तिवारणाय मात्रपदम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: प्रत्यभिज्ञायाम् Remembrance is defined by the Text: samskāra. Mental impression is reminiscent impression. [58. Mental impression is threefold: vega, bhāvanā, and sthitisthāpaka. Of these, only the bhāvanā variety is to be taken here] The word jñānam is inserted in the definition to avoid overapplicability in the destruction of mental impression (samskāradhvamsa).[59. Samskaradhvamsa is also born of samskāra, as without samskāra there can be no samskāra-dhvarmsa. Therefore, there is ativyāpti in the definition samskāramātra janyam. To avoid this, jñānam is added to the definition] The term born of samskāra is added (to the definition) to avoid overapplicability in perception of things like jar etc. To avoid overapplicability in reminiscence (pratyabhijñā), the term alone is used in the definition. [60. Identification of the cognition of a thing or a person with its or his recollection of past impression is pratyabhijñā reminiscence. Recognition thus results when immediate cognition arising from the contact of organ with the object of perception is identical with the recollection of past impressions. For instannce, when we say: verily, this is that man, this refers to the cognition arising from the immediate perception of man consequent on the contact of organ; and that refers to the recollection of past impressions relating to the same man. Thus, by inserting the term alone, the defect of overapplicability in reminiscence is avoided. Remembrance (smrti) is born of mental impression alone whereas pratyabhijñā is born out of perception also]
Verse 36
18. Apprehension (anubhava): तद्भिन्नं ज्ञानमनुभवः । स द्विविधः, यथार्थोऽयथार्थश्च ॥ १८ ॥
English Paraphrase
18. Knowledge other than recollection is apprehension. It is twofold: conformable to the object and not conformable to the object.
Sanskrit Commentary
अनुभवं लक्षयति । तद्भिन्नमिति । स्मृतिभिन्नं ज्ञानमनुभव इत्यर्थः । अनुभवं विभजते । स द्विविध इति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Apprehension is defined by the Text: Tadbhinnam. The meaning is that knowledge other than recollection is apprehension. Apprehension is divided by the Text: sa dvividhah.
Verse 37
19. Valid apprehension (Yathārtha anubhava): तद्वति तत्प्रकारकोऽनुभवो यथार्थः । यथा रजते इदं रजतमिति ज्ञानम् । सैव प्रमेत्युच्यते ॥ १९ ॥
English Paraphrase
19. A valid apprehension is that in which an object is known as possessing attributes which it really possesses eg the apprehension of silver arising in an object where there is silverness. This is also known as valid knowledge of a thing.
Sanskrit Commentary
यथार्थानुभवस्य लक्षणमाह । तद्वतीति । ननु घटे घटत्वम् इति प्रमायामव्याप्तिः, घटत्वे घटाभावादिति चेत् न, यत्र यत्संबन्धोऽस्ति तत्र तत्संबन्धानुभवः इत्यर्थाद्धटत्वे घटसंबन्धोऽस्तीति नाव्याप्तिः । सैवेति । यथार्थानुभव एव शास्त्रे प्रमेत्युच्यत इत्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The characteristic mark of valid apprehension is explained by the Text: Tadvati.... Objection: If the above definition is admitted, it results in partial applicability in true apprehension: potness in a pot; because, jar does not exist in its genus potness. [61. The idea is this: The above definition of pramā applies to a cognition of jar when we interpret tadvati to mean ghatavati; but this cannot apply to a cognition of ghatatva itself as existing in a ghata, because, ghatatva is not the adhikarana of ghata or any other thing. The expression ghate ghatatvam amounts to saying: ghata vrtti ghatatvam, potness residing in a pot. Vrttitvam or ādheyatā is in potness. Through the link, viz ādheyatā (the quality of residing), pot can be accepted to reside as prakāra in potness if adheyatā were accepted as a sambandha of pot and potness. But ādheyatā as such is not recognised as a sambandha (relation) at all. So, pot cannot reside in potness as a prakāra. Accordingly. ghatavati (ghatatve) ghata prakāraka jñānam does not happen. As such partial applicability in the true apprehension potness in a pot has to be accepted] Reply: It is not so; because where a certain relation exists, there also occurs the apprehension of that relation. [62. When tadvati is interpreted to mean tatsambandhavati, pot becomes the substratum of ghatatvasarmbandha, because ghatasambandha, exists in ghatatva; SO ghatatva becomes the adhikarana of ghatasambandha. Thus, tatsambandhavati (ghatasambandhavati ghatatve) tatprakāraka jñānam (ghataprakāraka jñānam) happens] Therefore there is no defect of avyāpti in the above definition. This valid cognition is also known as pramā in the scriptures.
Verse 38
20. Erroneous apprehension (ayathārtha anubhava): तदभाववति तत्प्रकारकोऽनुभवोऽयथार्थः । यथा शुक्ताविदंरजतमिति ज्ञानम् । सैव अप्रमा इत्युच्यते ॥ २० ॥
English Paraphrase
20. That is non-valid apprehension in which an object is known as having an attribute which it does not have in reality. For example, the apprehension of silver arising in a piece of mother-of-pearl. This indeed is called the false cognition.
Sanskrit Commentary
अयथार्थानुभवं लक्षयति । तदभाववतीति । नन्विदं संयोगीति प्रमायामतिव्याप्तिरिति चेत् न । यदवच्छेदेन यत्संबन्धाभावस्तदवच्छेदेन तत्संबन्धज्ञानस्य विवक्षितत्वात् । संयोगाभावावच्छेदेन संयोगज्ञानस्य भ्रमत्वात्संयोगावच्छेदेन संयोगसंबन्धस्य सत्त्वान्नातिव्याप्तिः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines erroneous cognition: tadabhāvavati... [63. Apramā anubhava is when a cognition has for its prakāra its inherent jāti while, in reality, the thing suffers from the absence of that very prakāra; eg the cognition of a snake. Snake must have the characteristic of snakeness in it in order to render that cognition valid. Suppose, what really exists before one is a rope and not a snake; the snakeness which is the prakāra in the above cognition is absent in a rope which has for its feature only ropeness. Hence, the cognition of snake is faulty inasmuch as there is no snakeness in it; hence it is apramā anubhava] Objection: The above definition brings in overapplicability in the apprehension: this is in conjunction.[64. The idea of the objection is this: conjunction possesses the characteristic of partial pervasiveness. Therefore, the adhikarana has both conjunction and also the negation of conjunction. For example, when one says, the monkey is sitting on a tree, one gets the knowledge that the tree is in conjunction with monkey. But the definition of apramā also applies to this apprehension; because, here we have kapi samyogaprakāraka anubhava in an adhikarana where there is also the absence of kapisamyoga in the lower part of the tree] Reply: No; it is only in erroneous apprehension that the knowledge of contact is obtained from a part where there is the absence of contact and what is implied here by apramā is the knowledge of contact in a particular space where there is the absence of contact. But in the above apprehension (kapisamyogīti jñāne), contact is known to exist in the space where there is the contact of monkey. So there is no defect of overapplicability.
Verse 39
21. Valid apprehension is fourfold: यथार्थानुभवः चतुर्विधः प्रत्यक्षानुमित्युपमितिशाब्दभेदात् ॥ २१ ॥
English Paraphrase
21. Valid apprehension is fourfold due to the division into perception, inference, analytical knowledge and verbal testimony.
Sanskrit Commentary
यथार्थानुभवं विभजते । यथार्थेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Valid apprehension is divided by the Text: Yathārtha....
Verse 40
22. The instruments of valid apprehension: तत् करणमपि चतुर्विधं प्रत्यक्षानुमानोपमानशब्द भेदात् ॥ २२ ॥
English Paraphrase
22. Their instruments too are fourfold due to division into the instrument of perception, instrument of inference, instrument of comparative knowledge; and the instrument of verbal testimony.
Sanskrit Commentary
प्रसङ्गात्प्रमाकरणं विभजते । तत्करणमपीति । प्रमाकरणमित्यर्थः । प्रमाकरणं प्रमाणमिति प्रमाणसामान्यलक्षणम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: In this context, the means of valid apprehension is also being explained.[65. Having mentioned valid apprehension, there arises the need to mention the means of valid apprehension. So, the means are being classified and explained by the Text] That which gives rise to right apprehension is proof; this definition is the general characteristic of proof.
Verse 41
23. Definition of Special cause: असाधारणं कारणं करणम् ॥ २३ ॥
English Paraphrase
23. The uncommon cause (instrumental or efficient) is the special cause.
Sanskrit Commentary
करणलक्षणमाह । असाधारणेति । दिक्कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय असाधारणेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The text defines cause: asādhārana.... The term uncommon is inserted in the definition to avoid the defect of overapplicability in time, space etc. [66. There are two types of causes: common cause - sādhārana kāraņa, and uncommon or special cause - asādhāraņa kārana. That which is the cause of every effect is the general or common cause, eg space, time, destiny etc. That which is the specific cause of an effect is called the special or uncommon cause, eg yarn in the production of cloth]
Verse 42
24. Definition of cause: कार्यनियतपूर्ववृत्ति कारणम् ॥ २४ ॥
English Paraphrase
24. That which invariably precedes its effect is its cause.
Sanskrit Commentary
कारणलक्षणमाह । कार्येति । पूर्ववृत्ति कारणमित्युक्ते रासभादावतिव्याप्तिः स्यादतो नियतेति । तावन्मात्रे कृते कार्येऽतिव्याप्तिरतः पूर्ववृत्तीति । ननु तन्तुरूपमपि पटं प्रति कारणं स्यादिति चेत् न, अनन्यथासिद्धत्वे सतीति विशेषणात् । अनन्यथासिद्धत्वमन्यथासिद्धिरहितत्वं । अन्यथासिद्धिः त्रिविधा - येन सहैव यस्य यं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वमवगम्यते तं प्रति तेन तदन्यथासिद्धम् । यथा तन्तुनां तन्तुरूपं तन्तुत्वं च पटं प्रति । अन्यं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वे ज्ञात एव यस्य यं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वमवगम्यते तं प्रति तदन्यथासिद्धम् । यथा शब्दं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वे ज्ञात एव पटं प्रत्याकाशस्य । अन्यत्र क्लृप्तनियतपूर्ववतिर्न एव कार्यसंभवे तत्सहभूतमन्यथासिद्धम् । यथा पाकजस्थले गन्धं प्रति रूपप्रागभावस्य । एवञ्च अनन्यथासिद्धनियतपूर्ववृत्तित्वं कारणत्वम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The text defines the cause; kārya... If cause is defined merely as antecedent, the defect of overapplicability occurs in animals like ass etc (in the production of a jar). This is avoided by the inclusion of the term invariably (as an ass need not be invariably antecedent in the production of a jar). Again, the mere definition kāryaniyatam kāraņam, brings in the defect of overapplicability in the produced effect. [67. That is, when so defined, it means that wherever there is product, there is product. Thus ativyapti occurs in kārya as niyatatva is found in effect also.] To avoid this, the term pūrvavṛtti is inserted in the definition (ie there can be no antecedent effect before the production of an effect). Objection: The colour of the yarn also becomes the cause of cloth (as both kāryaniyatatva and antecedence are found in colour). Reply: It is not so; because the definition of karana is qualified by the adjunct ananyathāsiddhatve sati which means provided, the antecedent thing is not connected with dispensable things. Ananyathāsiddha is that which does not depend for its invariable existence upon some other antecedent existing thing. [68. The idea is this: The absence of dispensable antecedent is anan yatāsiddha. There will not happen the absence of dispensable antecedents when there is the presence of indispensable cause; for example, the existence of ether and kapālarūpa etc in the production of a jar, are the dispensable antecedents. In these anyathāsiddhas, both invariability and antecedence are found, thus causing the defect of ativyāpti in the definition of karana. To avoid this, the term ananyathāsiddha is added to the definition] The absence of dispensable antecedents is ananyathäisiddha. Anyathāsiddhatva (the dispensable antecedence in the production of a thing) is threefold: (a) Things that are connected to inherent relation (samavāya sambandha) with the cause and thus become the antecedents to effect through them; as for instance, the colour of yarn (tanturūpa) and the generic attribute of yarn (tantutva) which are intimately united with yarn, are antecedent to its effect, viz, a piece of cloth (pata). Here both tanturūpa and tantutva are the examples for the first variety of anyathāsiddha. (b) Things that are antecedent to cause and therefore become antecedent to effect; as for instance, ether in the production of a jar. Ether is not directly perceived and its existence is proved only with reference to sound of which ether is the inherent cause. Thus, ether becomes antecedent to jar only after it becomes antecedent to its effect, sound. Therefore, ether is anyathāsiddha in the production of a jar. This is an example for the second type. (c) All other concomitants of cause that are not connected with it by inherence (samavāya). Since the production of an effect is determined by its invariable antecedence in some other place, other antecedents that accompany the product are considered anyathāsiddha: for instance, when heat is applied, the antecedent non-existence of colour (rūpa prāgabhāva) is anyathāsiddha in the production of smell. This is the third type. Thus a cause is defined as the indispensable invariable antecedence of an effect. [69. A mango, possessing all the qualities except smell, gets the quality of smell when subjected to heat. Here the causes of the production of odour in the fruit must be the antecedent non-existence of smell inasmuch as there cannot be any other antecedent non-existence except the antecedent non-existence of smell, in view of the maxim of the Naiyāyikas that without the existence of antecedent non-existence no effect can be produced; otherwise, we should be able to produce another jar from the clay of an already existing jar. Another mango possessing all the four qualities except colour and smell, is subjected to warming. In the process, the fruit gets both colour and smell. Here a doubt arises as to which antecedent non-existence is the cause in the production of smell. In places other than the application of heat, the antecedent non-existence of smell is determined to be the cause in the production of smell. Therefore, here also, the antecedent non-existence of smell is essential in the production of smell. Therefore, the antecedent non-existence of colour is anyathāsiddha here. This is the example for the third variety of anyathāsiddhatva]
Verse 43
25. Definition of effect: कार्यं प्रागभावप्रतियोगि ॥ २५ ॥
English Paraphrase
25. Effect is the counter-correlative of its own antecedent non-existence (in its inherent cause).
Sanskrit Commentary
कार्यलक्षणमाह । कार्यमिति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives the characteristic mark of effect: kāryam.... [70. Annambhatta defines effect - kārya as the Pratiyogin or counter correlative of its own antecedent non-existence. Pratiyogin is used in the context of either abhāva (absence or non-existence) or sambandha (relation). In the former, pratiyogin refers to the object whose absence is intended to be established. For ghalābhāva, the ghata is the pratiyogin of its abhāva. For the abhava of ghatābhāva, ghatābhāva is the pratiyogin. Effects like a pot, a table, etc come into being at a certain point of time which proves its antecedent non-existence as otherwise expressions such as 'will come into existence' would be redundant. So, before the effect comes into existence, there was its absence; this is known as antecedent non-existence or prāgabhāva and an effect like a ghata is the pratiyogin of such antecedent non-existence]
Verse 44
26. Cause (Kāranam): कारणं त्रिविधम् - समवाय्यसमवायिनिमित्तभेदात् ॥ २६ ॥ 27. The inherent cause (samavāyi kāraṇam): यत् समवेतं कार्यं उत्पद्यते तत् समवायिकारणम् । यथा तंतवः पटस्य, पटश्च स्वगतरूपादेः ॥ २७ ॥
English Paraphrase
26. Cause is of three kinds: inherent, non-inherent, and occasioning (instrumental or efficient). [71. Nyāya-Vaiśeşika School recognises that every positive effect has three causes, viz, samavāya, asamavāya, and nimitta; eg in a product like cloth, yarn is the inherent cause; the contact of two yarns is the non-inherent cause; and causes other than these two, like the weaver, shuttle etc are the efficient causes. Inherent cause is always a substance; non-inherent cause must be either a quality or an action and nothing else; while nimitta kāraņa may be of any kind. Abhāva has only nimitta kāraņa] 27. The inherent cause is that which is in inseparable union in (the thing in) which the effect is produced, as the yarns are of the (effect) cloth and the cloth is of the colour on it.
Sanskrit Commentary
कारणं विभजते । कारणमिति । समवायिकारणस्य लक्षणमाह — यत्समवेतमिति । यस्मिन् समवेतमित्यर्थः । असमवायिकारणं लक्षयति — कार्येणेति । कार्येणेत्येतदुदाहरति — तन्तुसंयोग इति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines inherent cause: Yat samavetam.... The idea is that it is in inseparable union with the effect. [72. Samavāyi kāraņa is defined as that inhering in which an effect emerges, eg the yarn which constitutes the intimate cause of its effect cloth, because the cloth is connected with the yarns by inherent union. Similarly, the colour on the piece of cloth being a quality, resides in the cloth by intimate relation. Therefore, the piece of cloth is the inherent cause of the colour on it]
Verse 45
28. The non-inherent cause (asamavāyi kāraṇam): कार्येण कारणेन वा सह एकस्मिन्नर्थे समवेतत्वे सति यत्कारणं तदसमवायिकारणम् । यथा तन्तुसंयोगः पटस्य, तन्तुरूपं पटरूपस्य ॥ २८ ॥
English Paraphrase
28. The non-inherent cause is one which is inseparably united in the same object either with the effect, or with the cause, as the conjunction of yarns in relation to the (effect) cloth or the colour of the yarns in relation to (the effect) the colour of the cloth. [73. Here taritusamyoga inheres in yarn with cloth which is the effect of the contact of yarns. Thus, both the cloth and the tantusamyoga exist in the same substratum, ie tantus. Therefore, tantusamyoga is the non-inherent cause to cloth]
Sanskrit Commentary
कार्येण पटेन सह एकस्मिंस्तन्तौ समवेतत्वात्तन्तुसंयोगः पटस्यासमवायिकारणमित्यर्थः । कारणेनेत्येतदुदाहरति - तन्तुरूपमिति । कारणेन पटेन सह एकस्मिंस्तंन्तौ समवेतत्वात्
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives two definitions of non-intimate cause: the first is: kāryeņa saha ekasminnarthe samavetatve sati yat kāraņam tat asamavāyikāraņam that is the non-inherent cause, which is inherent in the same substratum along with the effect. The Text provides the example of the relation of contact of yarn with cloth to explain this type of non-inherent cause. The second variety is: kāraṇena saha ekasminnarthe samavetatve sati yat kāraņam tat asamavāyikāraņam that is the non-inherent cause which is inherent in the substratum along with cloth which is the inherent cause of the colour of cloth (patarūpa). [74. The example cited here is the relationship of tanturūpa to patarūpa. Here the colour of yarns inheres in yarns along with cloth which is the inherent cause of patarūpa, that is to say, the colour of yarns produces the colour of cloth in cloth inhering in yarn along with cloth. Therefore, the colour of yarn is the non-inherent cause to the colour of cloth]
Verse 46
29. Occasioning cause (nimitta kāraṇam): तदुभयभिन्नं कारणं निमित्तकारणम् । यथा तुरीवेमादिकं पटस्य ॥ २९ ॥
English Paraphrase
29. The cause which is different from both these causes is the occasioning cause (instrumental or efficient), such as the shuttle, loom etc in the case of (production of) a piece of cloth.
Sanskrit Commentary
तन्तुरूपं पटरूपस्यासमवायिकारणमित्यर्थः । निमित्तकारणं लक्षयति तदुभयेति । समवाय्यसमवायिभिन्नकारणं निमित्तकारणमित्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines the occasioning cause: tadubhaya.... The cause different from both the intimate and non-intimate causes is the occasioning cause (either instrumental or efficient). [75. A cause which is other than either the samavāyi or the asamavāyi kāraṇa, is the nimitta kāraṇa, eg the activities of the weaver, the shuttle, the loom etc]
Verse 47
30. Special Cause (asādharanam kārannam): तदेत‌त्त्रिविधकारणमध्ये यदसाधारणं कारणं तदेव करणम् ॥ ३० ॥
English Paraphrase
30. That alone which is the uncommon cause among the three varieties of causes mentioned above, is the special cause.
Sanskrit Commentary
करणलक्षणमुपसंहरति तदेतदिति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text concludes the definition of special cause: Tadetat.... [76. Kāraṇa is defined by Annambhatta as an uncommon or special cause. Keśavamiśra in his Tarkabhāṣā defines karaṇam as sādhakatamam, ie the most immediate cause in bringing about an effect, that is to say, it is the most efficient cause. That which directly and immediately brings about the result by its own action is karaṇam, eg an arrow in killing a bird. Here, arrow is the special cause which brings about the instant death of a bird, because, it alone immediately brings about the result killing the bird by its own activity of striking the bird dead. Thus karaṇa is a cause which is most essential in producing a result and without the activity of which the effect is not produced in spite of the other causes being present. This is the view held by the modern Naiyāyikas. But the ancient Naiyāyikas define it as vyāpāravat asādhāraṇam kāraṇam karaṇam ie karaṇa is a special and operative cause. Vyāpāra is an operation which, not being a substance, is the product of a thing, and produces the effect of that thing. The example for this is as follows: The sense organ is karaṇa. The contact of a sense-organ with its corresponding object is the vyāpāra. The percept is the effect. The contact of sense-organs happens to be both cause and effect. It becomes cause with reference to the effect of organ and becomes also effect with reference to sense organ. That which is known as vyāpāra by the ancient Naiyāyikas is known as karaṇa by the modern Naiyāyikas]
Verse 48
SECTION IV THE NYAYA-VAISESIKA THEORY OF EPISTEMOLOGY 31. Perception (Pratyaksa Pramāņa): तत्र प्रत्यक्षज्ञानकरणं प्रत्यक्षम् ॥ ३१ ॥
English Paraphrase
31. Among them, perception is the special cause of percept.
Sanskrit Commentary
प्रत्यक्षलक्षणमाह तत्रेति । प्रमाणचतुष्टयमध्ये इत्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines percept: tatra.... Tatra means, among the four means of apprehension.
Verse 49
32. Perception defined and explained: इन्द्रियार्थसन्निकर्षजन्यं ज्ञानं प्रत्यक्षम् । त‌द्विविधं निर्विकल्पकं सविकल्पकं चेति । तत्र निष्प्रकारकं ज्ञानं निर्विकल्पकं यथेदं किश्चित् । सप्रकारकं ज्ञानं सविकल्पकं यथा डित्थोऽयं, ब्राह्मणोऽयं, श्यामोऽयं, पाचकोऽयमिति ॥ ३२ ॥
English Paraphrase
32. Perception is the apprehension born of the contact of the organ with an object. It is of two kinds: indeterminate and determinate. Of these, the knowledge of a thing without any attribute is known as the indeterminate apprehension; such as, this is something. Attributive knowledge is determinative apprehension. Apprehensions like, he is a Dittha, he is a Brahmaņa, he is black, he is a cook, are examples of determinate apprehension.
Sanskrit Commentary
प्रत्यक्षज्ञानस्य लक्षणमाह — इन्द्रियेति । इन्द्रियं चक्षुरादिकम्, अर्थो घटादिः, तयोः संनिकर्षः संयोगादिः, तज्जन्यं ज्ञानमित्यर्थः । तद्विभजते । तद्विविधमिति । निर्विकल्पकस्य लक्षणमाह — निष्प्रकारकमिति । विशेषणविशेष्यसंबन्धानवगाहि ज्ञानमित्यर्थः । ननु निर्विकल्पके किं प्रमाणमिति चेत् न, गौरिति विशिष्टज्ञानं विशेषणज्ञानजन्यं, विशिष्टज्ञानत्वात्, दण्डीति ज्ञानवत् इत्यनुमानस्य प्रमाणत्वात् । विशेषणज्ञानस्यापि सविकल्पकत्वेऽनवस्थाप्रसङ्गान्निर्विकल्पकसिद्धिः । सविकल्पकं लक्षयति । सप्रकारकमिति । नामजात्यादिविशेष्यविशेषणसम्बन्धावगाहि ज्ञानमित्यर्थः । सविकल्पकमुदाहरति । यथेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The distinguishing characteristic mark of perception is given in the Text: Indriya.... The organs are the eye etc. Objects are jar etc. Their connection is known as contact. That which is produced as a result of this contact (of organ with the object) is perception. It is again divided by the Text: it is of two kinds. The Text then gives the characteristic mark of indeterminate apprehension. The knowledge in which there is the absence (anavagahi) of a subject, attribute, and relation (between the attribute and the subject) is the indeterminate knowledge. Question: What is the proof to determine the indeterminate knowledge? Reply: The attributive knowledge: this is a cow, is born of the knowledge of the qualifying attribute cowness; because, it is an attributive knowledge (viśistajñāna) like the knowledge, A man with a stick. Inference is thus the proof (in establishing the indeterminate knowledge). If we say that the knowledge of the attribute also is determinate knowledge, it will then and us in the logical anomaly of infinitum regressus. Therefore, the knowledge of the attribute proved by inference, has to be accepted to determine the indeterminate knowledge. The knowledge which comprehends the relation of the qualified and the qualifier such as name, class etc is known as the determinate knowledge. Determinate knowledge is exemplified: yathā.... The Text defines determinate knowledge: Saprakārakam.... [77. The four examples given in the text are in illustration of samjñā, name; jāti class; guṇa quality; and kriyā - action]
Verse 50
33. The operative cause for perception is six-fold: प्रत्यक्षज्ञानहेतुरिन्द्रियार्थसंनिकर्षः षड्विधः । संयोगः, संयुक्तसम- वायः, संयुक्तसमवेतसमवायः, समवायः, समवेतसमवायः, विशेषणविशेष्यभावश्चेति ॥ ३३ ॥
English Paraphrase
33. The conjunction of organs with objects which causes perception is of six kinds: (a) conjunction; (b) inherent union with what has come into contact; (c) inherent union with what is intimately united with a thing which has come in contact; (d) inherent union; (e) inherent union with the intimately united; and (f) the connection of the attribute with the substantive.
Sanskrit Commentary
इन्द्रियार्थसंनिकर्ष विभजते । प्रत्यक्षेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text divides conjunction of organ with object: pratyaksa....
Verse 51
34. The first operative cause (Samyoga - sannikarsah): चक्षुषा घटप्रत्यक्षजनने संयोगः सन्निकर्षः ॥ ३४ ॥
English Paraphrase
34. Conjunction is the contact, producing perception of the jar by the eye.
Sanskrit Commentary
संयोगसंनिकर्षमुदाहरति । चक्षुषेति । द्रव्यप्रत्यक्षे सर्वत्र संयोगः संनिकर्षः । आत्मा मनसा संयुज्यते, मन इन्द्रियेण, इन्द्रियमर्थन, ततः प्रत्यक्षज्ञानमुत्पद्यते इत्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text exemplifies conjunctive contact: cakṣuṣā.... Conjunctive contact is the cause in producing perception of substances in all cases. The self (ātman) comes in contact with mind (manas), the mind with the organ (indriya), and organ with the object (visaya), and then perceptual knowledge takes place. [78. According to the Nyāya doctrine, direct perception is always dependent on some relation between an Indriya (sense organ) and an artha (object). This relation is the operative cause in bringing about a cognition. The cognition may either be laukika (ordinary) perception of bhāva padārthas (positive entities) like that of a dravya, guṇa, karma or jāti. Again, we may have perception of a quality like śabda in ākāśa which is not perceived by the senses; there occurs also the perception of the universal soundness abiding in sound. We may have also perception of negative entities like, the absence of a pot or a table etc. In each case what is necessary is a certain kind of relation between the artha (object) perceived and the respective indriya (sense organ) involved in the act of perception. This perception is again twofold: Savikalpaka (determinant) and Nirvikalpaka (indeterminant) which has been dealt with earlier]
Verse 52
35. Second operative cause (Samyukta samavāya): घटरूपप्रत्यक्षजनने संयुक्त समवायः संनिकर्षः । चक्षुः संयुक्ते घटे रूपस्य समवायात् ॥ ३५ ॥
English Paraphrase
35. The inherent union with the conjoint is the contact in producing the perception of the colour of a jar, as the colour is inherently united with the jar which is in contact with the eye. [79. The colour is intimately united with the jar which is conjointed with the organ. Hence the contact of the eye with the colour of the jar is samyukta samavāya]
Sanskrit Commentary
संयुक्तसमवायमुदाहरति । घटरूपेति । तत्र युक्तिमाह । चक्षुःसंयुक्त इति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Inherent union with the conjoint is exemplified: ghațarūpa.... The process is explained: cakșuh samyukte.
Verse 53
36. Third operative cause (Samyukta-samaveta-samavāya): रूपत्वसामान्यप्रत्यक्षे संयुक्तसमवेतसमवायः सन्निकर्षः । चक्षुः संयुक्ते घटे रूपं समवेतं, तत्र रूपत्वस्य समवायात् ॥ ३६ ॥
English Paraphrase
36. Inherent union with the intimately united is the contact in producing the perception of the universal genus colourness, as colour is inherently united with the jar that is conjoint with the ocular organ, and the genus colourness is inherently united therewith.
Sanskrit Commentary
संयुक्तसमवेतसमवायमुदाहरति । रूपत्वेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives an example of inherent union with the intimately united - rūpatva.... [80. Here, eye is in contact with the jar with which is intimately united the colour of the jar in which inheres its genus. Ghatarūpatva is, therefore, perceived by the eye by means of the contact samyukta-samavetasamavāya]
Verse 54
37. Fourth operative cause (Samavāyaḥ sannikarsah): श्रोत्रेण शब्दसाक्षात्कारे समवायः सन्निकर्षः कर्णविवरवर्त्याकाशस्य श्रोत्रत्वात् शब्दस्याकाशगुणत्वात् गुणगुणिनोश्च समवायात् ॥ ३७ ॥
English Paraphrase
37. Inherent union is the contact in the perception of word by the organ of hearing, as the organ of hearing is the ether in the cavity of the ear inasmuch as sound is the quality of ether and the quality and the qualified are inherently united.
Sanskrit Commentary
समवायमुदाहरति । श्रोत्रेणेति । तदुपपादयति । कर्णेति । ननु दूरस्थशब्दस्य कथं श्रोत्रसम्बन्ध इति चेत् न, वीचीतरङ्गन्यायेन कदम्बमुकुलन्यायेन वा शब्दान्तरोत्पत्तिक्रमेण श्रोत्रदेशे जातस्य श्रोत्रेण संबन्धात्प्रत्यक्षसंभवः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives an example of inherent union: śrotrena. That is being elaborated Karna.... Question: How does a distant sound come in contact with the auditory sense? Reply: Just like the principle of vīcītaranga or kadambamukula; the first sound produces another sound which in turn propels another, and so on, till the last sound reaches the auditory sense, thus enabling one to perceive sound directly. [81. Vīcītaranga nyāya is the principle of a wave urging forward another wave. In the ocean one wave propels another, till the first and all the subsequent waves reach the shore. In the same way, there is a successive operation in the case of the production of sound till they reach the auditory orifice. Kadambamukula nyāya indicates the simultaneous blossoming forth of the buds in a kadamba tree when a cloud thunders in the sky]
Verse 55
38. Fifth operative cause - Samaveta-samavāya-sannikarsah: शब्दत्वसाक्षात्कारे समवेतसमवायः सन्निकर्षः श्रोत्रसमवेते शब्दे शब्दत्वस्य समवायात् ॥ ३८ ॥
English Paraphrase
38. Inherent union with the inherently united is the contact in cognising soundness, as the genus soundness is inherently united with sound which is inherently united with the auditory sense.
Sanskrit Commentary
समवेतसमवायमुदाहरति । शब्दत्वेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives an example of inherent union with the intimately united: śabdatva....
Verse 56
39. Sixth operative cause (Viśeṣana Sannikarsa): Viśeşyabhāva अभावप्रत्यक्षे विशेषणविशेष्यभावः सन्निकर्षः, घटाभाव- वद्भूतलमित्यत्र चक्षुः संयुक्ते भूतले घटाभावस्य विशेषणत्वात् ॥ ३९ ॥
English Paraphrase
39. The connection of the qualifier and the qualified is the conjunction in the perception of negation, as in the cognition: This spot of earth is with the negation of jar. Here, the negation of a jar is an attribute of a place in contact with the eye.
Sanskrit Commentary
विशेषणविशेष्यभावमुदाहरति । अभावेति । तदुपपादयति । घटाभाववदिति । भूतले घटो नास्तीत्यत्र अभावस्य विशेष्यत्वं द्रष्टव्यम् । एतेन अनुपलब्धेः प्रमाणान्तरत्वं निरस्तम् । यद्यत्र घटोऽभविष्यत्तर्हि भूतलमिवाद्रक्ष्यत, दर्शनाभावान्नास्तीति तर्कितप्रतियोगिसत्त्वविरोध्यनुपलब्धिसहकृतेन्द्रियेणैव अभावज्ञानोत्पत्तौ अनुपलब्धेः प्रमाणान्तरत्वासंभवात् । अधिकरणज्ञानार्थमपेक्षणीयेन्द्रियस्यैव करणत्वोपपत्तावनुपलब्धेः करणत्वायोगात् । विशेषणविशेष्यभावो विशेषणविशेष्यस्वरूपमेव, नातिरिक्तः संबन्धः । प्रत्यक्षज्ञानमुपसंहरंस्तस्य करणमाह । एवमिति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives an example of the connection of the attribute and the subject: abhāvah.... This is being explained: Ghatābhāvavat.... In the cognition this spot of earth is with the negation of jar, the spot of earth is the qualified and the negation of jar is the attribute. [82. In this cognition, the eye is in contact with the spot of earth of which the negation of jar is the attribute, and, therefore, here, the connection is, contact with the attribute] In the cognition, there is no jar on the spot of earth, (where the eye is in contact with the spot of earth of which) the negation of jar is the qualified. [83. Hence, here, the connection is, contact with the qualified. Both these cognitions though differing in form, are identical in meaning. They are conjointly expressed in the Text as viśeṣaṇaviśeşya bhāva. With the contact of the eye with the spot of earth in the above two cognitions, the negation either on account of its possessing the property of Viśeşya or of Viśeṣaṇa becomes perceived on the spot of earth. The relation between the spot of earth and the negation of pot is no other than Visesana-Viśeşya bhāva] By this, the necessity for accepting non-perception as a distinct source of knowledge (anupalabdheḥ pramānāntarat- vam) is refuted. If there had been a jar, then the jar would have been apprehended as existing on that spot of earth. It is due to its absence, the apprehension, jar is not here, arises. Here, in this argument, is found the counter-entity of the negation of apprehension, thus proving that non-perception is only an accessory (sahakarn) of the eye which alone perceives the negation of jar on earth. Therefore (in this knowledge of the negation of jar on earth), the eye alone being the cause of the knowledge of substratum of negation it is impossible to state that anupalabdhi (non-perception) is an independent source to gain the knowledge of negation of jar.[84. The Nyaya School of thought does not accept the necessity of postulating a distinct source of valid knowledge (pramāṇa) to cognise absence (abhāva) as this could as well be achieved by perception in which the operative causal relation is between the viśeṣaṇa and viśeşya. The absence is apprehended on the same locus (adhikaraṇa). And this perception of abhāva of a certain thing on a certain locus, is not to be taken to be merely due to the defect of the eye or due to insufficient light; but it ought to be preceded by Tarka, that is to say, by the thought like the following: if a pot were on the ground, I should be seeing it right now, but I do not see the pot. This Tarka brings in the awareness of the absence of the pot on the ground. It is in order to emphasize such effective perception of absence that Annambhatta mentions in his Dīpikā about the existence of the pratiyogi (counter entity) of the negation of apprehension. This further proves that non-perception is necessary for the immediate apprehension of abhāva; here, non-apprehension is an auxiliary condition (sahakari) of the eye which alone perceives the ghatābhāva on a spot of earth. Therefore, admission of a separate pramāņa like anupalabdhi is redundant when the same can be had by pratyaksa — this is the Naiyāyika stand-point] The relationship between the qualifier and the qualified is nothing but the nature of the attribute and the qualified. By this no other relationship is meant.
Verse 57
40. Conclusion: एवं सन्निकर्षषट्कजन्यं ज्ञानं प्रत्यक्षं, तत्करणमिन्द्रियम्, तस्मादिन्द्रियं प्रत्यक्षप्रमाणमिति सिद्धम् ॥ ४० ॥
English Paraphrase
40. Perception is thus the cognition caused by the sixfold contact, and the organ is its cause (efficient or instrumental). Therefore, organ is the proof of perception.
Sanskrit Commentary
असाधारणकारणत्वादिन्द्रियं प्रत्यक्षज्ञानकरणमित्यर्थः ।प्रत्यक्षप्रमाणमुपसंहरति । तस्मादिति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Concluding the section dealing with perceptual knowledge, the Text mentions its special cause: evam.... Since the organ is the special cause of perception, it is called the efficient or instrumental cause of perception. He concludes the section dealing with pratyakṣa pramāṇa by the words: tasmāt....
Verse 58
SECTION V INFERENCE 1. Inference (anumānam): अनुमितिकरणमनुमानम् ॥ १ ॥
English Paraphrase
1. Inference is the special cause of inferential knowledge.
Sanskrit Commentary
अनुमानं लक्षयति-अनुमितिकरणमिति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text defines inference: anumitikaranam. [85. Inference is treated after direct perception inasmuch as the former depends on the latter]
Verse 59
2. Inferential knowledge (anumiti): परामर्शजन्यं ज्ञानमनुमितिः ॥ २ ॥
English Paraphrase
2. Inferential knowledge is the knowledge born of subsumptive reflection.
Sanskrit Commentary
अनुमितिं लक्षयति -- परामर्शेति । ननु संशयोत्तरप्रत्यक्षेऽतिव्याप्तिः स्थाणुपुरुषसंशयानन्तरं पुरुषत्वव्याप्यकरादिमानयम् इति परामर्शे सति पुरुष एव इति प्रत्यक्षजननात् । न च तत्रानुमितिरेवेति वाच्यम् । पुरुषं साक्षात्करोमि इत्यनुव्यवसायविरोधादिति चेत् न, पक्षतासहकृतपरामर्शजन्यत्वस्य विवक्षितत्वात् । सिषाधयिषाविरहविशिष्टसिद्धयभावः पक्षता । साध्यसिद्धिरनुमितिप्रतिबन्धिका । सिद्धिसत्त्वेऽपि अनुमिनुयाम् इतीच्छायामनुमितिदर्शनात् सिषाधयिषोत्तेजिका । ततश्चोत्तेजकाभावविशिष्टमण्यभावस्य दाहकारणत्ववत्सिषाधयिषाविरहविशिष्टसिद्धयभावस्याप्यनुमितिकारणत्वम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text gives the characteristic mark of inferential knowledge: parāmarśa.... Objection: There is overapplicability in perception in the form of a conclusion arrived at after first doubting, as in the example: Is this a post or a man? (On the perception of an object from a distance in insufficient light, the doubt, 'is this a post or a man?' arises). Then he reasons with the help of subsumptive reflection: But this object has hands etc which are found invariably associated with mankind. Then arises the perception: this indeed is a man. It should not be argued that the above cognition this indeed is a man is to be construed as inferential knowledge inasmuch as it has been derived consequent on parāmarśa, viz, this has hands etc which is vyāpya (less pervasive) with regard to mankind, and as it is contrary to anuvyavasaya, like: I am perceiving a man. [86. By raising the above objection the opponent means to say that in samsayottara pratyaksa a kind of intermediary parāmarśa operates inasmuch as perceptual experience arises following consideration of certain particulars of the object, thus causing the defect of ativyāpti in samsayottara pratyaksa. This defect in the definition is avoided by prefixing parāmarsa with the adjunct, pakṣatāsahakṛta] Reply: Not so; because, the defect in the definition is avoided by prefixing parāmarśa with the adjunct: in association with pakṣatā. Then it ought to be interpreted as follows: inferential cognition is the knowledge that arises from parāmarśa in association with paksatā. Paksatā (ie the characteristic of an instance) consists in having in it the negation of the ascertainment of the thing to be inferred, accompanied by a negation of any desire to infer an already proven thing [87. In samśayottara pratyaksa, there is no paksatājanyatva, because, man and his hands etc are perceived simultaneously, and as such, no sādhya is left to be ascertained, and also there could be no desire to infer. Thus the defect of ativyāpti is removed. Again, if it is merely said, parāmarśājanyam anumitih, the defect of ativyāpti occurs in parāmarsadhvamsa, as it is also parāmarśajanya. To avoid this, the term jñānam is inserted in the definition.] Indubious knowledge of the thing to be proved is the counter-acting factor in inferential knowledge (ie when the thing to be proved is ascertained in the paksa, the need to infer its existence is superfluous). Even when the thing to be proved is perceived, sometimes it is observed that a desire: I shall infer, stimulates one to take to inference (to establish the proven). Here, the factor that stimulates one (to take to inference) is the desire to establish a thing in the paksa. Just as the absence of a pratibandhaka mani (the gem capable of neutralising the burning power of fire) in association with the absence of a uttejaka mani (a gem that stimulates the power of burning in fire) is the cause of heat, even so, the absence of indubious knowledge of the thing to be proved in association with the absence of a desire to predicate a thing in the paksa is the cause of inferential knowledge. [88. When a particular variety of gem capable of neutralising the burning power of fire is placed near fire, the gem obstructs the burning power of fire; but, if some stimulator gem is placed near the counter-acting agent, it effectively neutralises the latter. Here, the absence of a desire to establish a thing — sişādhayişāvirahatva is given as a delimiting adjunct — viśeşana to indubious knowledge — siddhi, and not to the absence of indubious knowledge. The absence of stimulating-gem is given as a delimiting adjunct to the obstructor-gem. When the obstructor-gem is present in association with the stimulator-gem, there will be the power of burning in fire even like power of burning remaining intact when there is the absence of obstructor-gem in association with the absence of stimulator-gem. Similarly, of the two, viz, (1) indubious knowledge, and, (2) a desire to predicate a thing in the paksa, the former is the obstructing agent — pratibandhaka, while the latter is the stimulator agent — uttejaka. Inferential knowledge is brought about consequent on the absence of indubious knowledge in association with the absence of a desire to establish or predicate a thing]
Verse 60
3. Subsumptive reflection (parāmarśa): व्याप्तिविशिष्टपक्षधर्मताज्ञानं परामर्शः । यथा वह्निव्याप्यधूमवानयं पर्वत इति ज्ञानं परामर्शः । तज्जन्यं पर्वतो वह्निमानिति ज्ञानमनुमितिः ॥ ३ ॥
English Paraphrase
3. Subsumptive reflection is the knowledge of reason existing in the subject accompanied by invariable concomitance (of sādhya), eg the knowledge: this mountain has smoke which is invariably pervaded by fire is parāmarsa; and the cognition that results from it and takes the form: the mountain is fiery is the inferential knowledge.
Sanskrit Commentary
परामर्श लक्षयति व्याप्तीति । व्याप्तिविषयकं यत्पक्षधर्मताज्ञानं स परामर्श इत्यर्थः । परामर्शमभिनीय दर्शयति यथेति । अनुमितिमभिनीय दर्शयति तज्जन्यमिति । परामर्शजन्यमित्यर्थः । व्याप्तिलक्षणमाह यत्रेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Author defines parāmarśa: vyāpti.... The meaning is that the knowledge of the existence of reason in an instance which has invariable concomitance as its object, is subsumptive reflection. The Author explains parāmarśa by declaration through the words: Yathā.... Similarly, he explains inferential knowledge through the usage of words, tajjanyam.... The meaning is: inferential knowledge is born of subsumptive reflection.
Verse 61
4. Invariable concomitance - vyāpti: यत्र यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निरिति साहचर्यनियमो व्याप्तिः ॥ ४ ॥
English Paraphrase
4. Invariable concomitance is the certainty of co-existence like, wherever there is smoke there is fire.
Sanskrit Commentary
यत्र धूमस्तत्राग्निः इति व्याप्तेरभिनयः । साहचर्यनियमः इति लक्षणम् । साहचर्य सामानाधिकरण्यं, तस्य नियमः । हेतुसमानाधिकरणात्यन्ताभावाप्रतियोगिसाध्यसामाना- धिकरण्यं व्याप्तिरित्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The characteristic mark of vyāpti is given in the Text: yatra.... The Text further illustrates vyāpti through the words: where there is smoke there is also fire. The distinguishing characteristic mark of invariable concomitance is the invariability of concomitance. Concomitance is co-existence (of smoke and fire) in one and the same substratum; its certainty is niyama. The fuller definition of vyāpti is: the quality of being co-existent in the same substratum with the thing to be proved which does not become the counter-entity of its own absolute negation that co-exists with reason in the same substratum. [89. Take for example, smoke and fire. The said vyapti becomes applicable to smoke. What could be the negation that co-exists in the mountain with the reason, smoke? It can be the negation of pot, but not the negation of fire, because where there is smoke, there is also fire. Fire thus cannot become the counter-entity of its negation. With that fire (which does not become the counter-entity to its own negation that co-exists with reason, smoke) there is co-existence to smoke. So, the said vyāpti is in the smoke)]
Verse 62
5. Paksadharmata - The special feature of a subject: व्याप्यस्य पर्वतादिवृत्तित्वं पक्षधर्मता ॥ ५ ॥
English Paraphrase
5. The presence of an invariably concomitant thing in an object like a mountain makes it the characteristic of a receptacle or subject. [90. When fire is intended to be established on a hill because of the presence of smoke, it is a valid inference. Here, fire is the sādhya, (probandum) hill is the paksa (or subject) and smoke is the hetu or sadhana (probans). The perception of smoke on the hill leads to the inference of existence of fire on the hill, that is to say, on the perception of hetu on the paksa, the sādhya is inferred. But, this perception of hetu (smoke) on the paksa (hill) leads to correct inference of the existence of sādhya (fire) only if the hetu in question is invariably associated with sādhya everywhere. So, it is not the mere presence of smoke by itself that leads to inference; it must be smoke as qualified by the invariable association with fire. This relation is vyapti and the smoke is the vyāpya, ie that which is characterised by vyapti. So, pakşadharmata (the special feature of the paksa hill) is defined as the presence of vyāpya (smoke) in the paksa (hill)]
Sanskrit Commentary
पक्षधर्मतास्वरूपमाह - व्याप्यस्येति ।
English Translation
Dipika:
Verse 63
6. Inference is twofold: अनुमानं द्विविधं - स्वार्थं परार्थं च ॥ ६ ॥
English Paraphrase
6. Inference is of two kinds: for oneself and for Another.
Sanskrit Commentary
-_-
English Translation
-_-
Verse 64
7. (a) Inference for oneself: तत्र स्वार्थं स्वानुमितिहेतुः, तथाहि, स्वयमेव भूयोदर्शनेन यत्र यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निरिति महानसादौ व्याप्तिं गृहीत्वा पर्वतसमीपं गतः, तद्गते चाग्नौ सन्दिहानः पर्वते धूमं पश्यन् व्याप्तिं स्मरति यत्र यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निः इति । तदनन्तरं वह्निव्याप्यधूमवानयं पर्वत इति ज्ञानमुत्पद्यते, अयमेव लिङ्गपरामर्श इत्युच्यते । तस्मात् पर्वतो वह्निमानिति ज्ञानमनुमितिः उत्पद्यते । तदेतत् स्वार्थानुमानम् ॥ ७ ॥
English Paraphrase
7. Of them, inference for oneself is the cause of one's own inferential knowledge. For instance, one, having repeatedly seen in the hearth etc the invariable concomitance of smoke with fire, arrives at the universal generalisation: wherever there is smoke there is fire, and goes near a mountain. On suspecting fire on the mountain, and seeing smoke thereon, he remembers the universal concomitance: wherever there is smoke there is fire. After this, there dawns in him the knowledge this mountain is smoky, pervaded by fire. This is known as subsumptive reflection arising from a mark. From the above knowledge, the inferential knowledge, mountain has fire in it arises. This is one's own inference.
Sanskrit Commentary
अनुमानं विभजते अनुमानमिति । स्वार्थानुमानं दर्शयति स्वयमेवेति । ननु पार्थिवत्वलोहलेख्यत्वादौ शतशः सहचारदर्शनेऽपि वज्रमणौ व्यभिचारोपलब्धेर्भूयोदरनिन कथं व्याप्तिग्रह इति चेत् न । व्यभिचारज्ञानविरहसहकृतसहचारज्ञानस्य व्याप्तिग्राहकत्वात् । व्यभिचारज्ञानं निश्चयः शङ्का च । तद्विरहः कचित्तर्कात्, कचित्स्वतः सिद्ध एव । धूमाग्न्योर्व्याप्तिग्रहे कार्यकारणभावभङ्गप्रसङ्गलक्षणस्तर्को व्यभिचारशङ्कानिवर्तकः । ननु सकलवह्निधूमयोरसन्निकर्षात्कथं व्याप्तिग्रह इति चेत् न । वह्नित्वधूमत्वरूप- सामान्यप्रत्यासत्त्यासकलवह्निधूमज्ञानसंभवात् । तस्मादिति । लिङ्गपरामर्शादित्यर्थः ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: Tatra in the Text means between the two kinds of inference. Bhuyodarśanena in the Text means acceptance of the invariable concomitance of smoke with fire after repeated ascertainment of invariable co-existence of the thing to be proved and the reason. Objection: One may observe a hundred times the co-existence of earth and the possibility of its being scratched by metals; still, in things like diamond, a deviation to the above rule is observed (ie though vajra is a particular modification of earth, no scratches can be caused by any metal except vajra). Then, how can it be said that invariable concomitance is ascertained by repeated observation? [91. The opponent is questioning the sustainability of the statement: one having repeatedly seen in the hearth etc the invariable concomitance of smoke with fire, arrives at the certain generalization: wherever there is smoke there is fire. This statement is wrong inasmuch as there exists a deviation in things like vajra. In the inference, wherever there is pārthivatva there is lohalekhyatva', pārthivatva is vyāpya and lohalekhyatva is vyāpaka. This vyāpti jñāna is gained by repeated perception of their co-existence. But this vyāptijñāna, gained by repeated observation, is defective inasmuch as it is vitiated from vyabhicāra dosa, ie presence of hetu in a place where there is the absence of sādhya. Here, in vajra, pārthivatva, is present but lohalekhyatva is absent, ie hetu is present where there is the absence of sadhya. Therefore, bhūyodarśana cannot be accepted as the right means to establish vyāpti] Reply: The objection is not tenable, because, vyāpti is to be interpreted as the knowledge of co-existence in association with an absence of fallacious knowledge. Fallacious knowledge is twofold; certainty of a contrary fact, and mere suspicion. The absence of vyabhicarajñāna is proved sometimes by reasoning; sometimes it is self-proved from lack of reason. Vyabhicāra śaikā in the statement of the invariable concomitance of smoke and fire is removed by the argument of the possibility of breaking the relation of effect and cause. [92. The meaning is this: the suspicion, why not there be smoke, though no fire exists? is untenable since it breaks the law of relation of cause and effect between fire and smoke. Smoke is the effect of fire. So, there is kāryakāraṇa bhāva between smoke and fire. Therefore, entertainment of vyabhicāra śańkā in a context like this is untenable] Objection: How can this invariable concomitance of smoke and fire be accepted when there is the absence of perception of all fires and all smokes existing everywhere? Reply: One can have the knowledge of all types of fires and smokes existing in all the places by means of perception of the generic attributes of fire and smoke. Tasmāt in the text means from linga parāmarsa, ie from the subsumptive reflection arising from a mark.
Verse 65
8. b) Inference for others: यत्तु स्वयं धूमादग्निमनुमाय परंप्रतिबोधयितुं पञ्चावयव वाक्यं प्रयुज्यते तत् परार्थानुमानम् । यथा पर्वतो वह्निमान्, धूमत्वात्, यो यो धूमवान् स वह्निमान् यथा महानसः, तथा चायं, तस्मात्तथेति । अनेन प्रतिपादितात् लिङ्गात् परोऽप्यग्निं प्रतिपद्यते ॥ ८ ॥
English Paraphrase
8. But when, one, having inferred for oneself fire from smoke, employs the five-membered syllogism to make others understand the same, it is known as 'inference for others', eg the mountain is fiery, because of smoke; whichever is smoky is fiery, as a hearth, and this is such, and therefore it is so. From this five membered syllogism, others also apprehend fire (on the mountain by means of inference).
Sanskrit Commentary
परार्थानुमानमाह - यत्त्विति । यच्छब्दस्य 'तत्परार्थानुमानम्' इति तच्छब्देनान्वयः । पञ्चावयववाक्यमुदाहरति - यथेति ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text explains 'inference for another': yat tu.... The word yat has to be taken in agreement with the word tat as by the word yat (that) inference for another is meant. The five membered syllogism is exemplified: yathā....
Verse 66
9. The five-membered syllogism प्रतिज्ञा - हेतु - उदाहरण - उपनय - निगमनानि पञ्चावयवाः । पर्वतो वह्निमानिति प्रतिज्ञा । धूमवत्वात् इति हेतुः । यो यो धूमवान् स वह्निमान् यथा महानस इत्युदाहरणम् । तथा च अयमिति उपनयः । तस्मात्तथेति निगमनम् ॥ ९ ॥
English Paraphrase
9. The five members of the syllogism are: the proposition set down; the reason adduced therein; the example; the subsumptive correlative; and the conclusion. The proposition set down is, the mountain is fiery, the reason adduced in support of the above proposition is, due to smokiness; the example cited is, whichever is smoky is fiery, as a hearth; the subsumptive correlative is, and so this mountain (has smoke pervaded by fire); therefore it is such is the conclusion.
Sanskrit Commentary
अवयवस्वरूपमाह प्रतिज्ञेति ॥ उदाहृतवाक्ये प्रतिज्ञादिविभागमाह पर्वतो वह्निमानिति ॥ साध्यवत्तया पक्षवचनं प्रतिज्ञा । पञ्चम्यन्तं लिङ्गप्रतिपादकं हेतुः । व्याप्तिप्रतिपादकं उदाहरणम् । व्याप्तिविशिष्टलिङ्गप्रतिपादकं वचनमुपनयः । हेतुसाध्यवत्तया पक्षप्रतिपादकं वचनं निगमनम् । अबाधितत्वादिकं निगमनप्रयोजनम् ।
English Translation
Dīpikā: The Text expalins the nature of the limbs: pratijñā.... Through sentences of examples the Text explains the sections like pratijña etc: parvato vahnimān.... The declaration of the subject (receptable paksa in which the sādhya is to be ascertained) along with the thing to be proved is proposition. The declaration indicating the mark that serves to demonstrate the sādhya in the paksa and which (ie the declaration) ends in the ablative case, is the reason. The declaration which ascertains the invariable concomitance of reason and the thing to be proved, is the example. The declaration of mark accompanied by the invariable concomitance of the thing to be proved is the subsumptive correlative.
